class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Nerva%E2%80%93Antonine_dynasty" title="Template:Nerva&#8211;Antonine dynasty"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Nerva%E2%80%93Antonine_dynasty&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Antoninus Pius</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#716;/: secondary stress follows">&#716;</span><span title="/&#230;/: \'a\' in \'bad\'">&#230;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/a&#618;/: \'i\' in \'tide\'">a&#618;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span></span> <wbr/><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="/a&#618;/: \'i\' in \'tide\'">a&#618;</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Titus Aelius Hadrianus Antoninus Augustus Pius</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> 19 September 86&#160;&#8211; 7 March 161), also known as <b>Antoninus</b>, was <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> from 138 to 161. He was one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Good_Emperors" title="Five Good Emperors">Five Good Emperors</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nerva%E2%80%93Antonine_dynasty" title="Nerva&#8211;Antonine dynasty">Nerva&#8211;Antonine dynasty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aurelia_(gens)" title="Aurelia (gens)">Aurelii</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._150_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._150-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Born into a senatorial family, Antoninus held various offices during the reign of emperor <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>, acquiring favor which saw him adopted as Hadrian\'s son and successor shortly before Hadrian\'s death. He acquired the <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">name</a> <a href="/wiki/Pius" title="Pius">Pius</a> after his accession to the throne, either because he compelled the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_(ancient_Rome)" title="Imperial cult (ancient Rome)">deify</a> his adoptive father <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> or because he had saved senators sentenced to death by Hadrian in his later years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>His reign is notable for the peaceful state of the Empire, with no major revolts or military incursions during this time, and for his governing without ever leaving Italy. A successful military campaign in southern Scotland early in his reign resulted in the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a>. Antoninus was an effective administrator, leaving his successors a large surplus in the treasury, expanding free access to drinking water throughout the Empire, encouraging legal conformity, and facilitating the enfranchisement of freed slaves.\n</p><p>He died of illness in 161 and was succeeded by his adopted sons <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a> as co-emperors.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Childhood_and_family"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Childhood and family</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Marriage_and_children"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Marriage and children</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Favor_with_Hadrian"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Favor with Hadrian</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Emperor"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Emperor</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#A_non-military_reign"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">A non-military reign</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Economy_and_administration"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Economy and administration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Legal_reforms"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Legal reforms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Diplomatic_mission_to_China"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Diplomatic mission to China</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#In_later_scholarship"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">In later scholarship</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Descendants"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Descendants</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1"><a href="#Nerva&#8211;Antonine_family_tree"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Nerva&#8211;Antonine family tree</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_family">Childhood and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>He was born as the only child of <a href="/wiki/Titus_Aurelius_Fulvus" title="Titus Aurelius Fulvus">Titus Aurelius Fulvus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consul" title="Consul">consul</a> in 86,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._150_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._150-4">[4]</a></sup> whose family came from <a href="/wiki/Nemausus" title="Nemausus">Nemausus</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/N%C3%AEmes" title="N&#238;mes">N&#238;mes</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._523_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._523-7">[7]</a></sup> Titus Aurelius Fulvius was the son of a senator of the same name, who, as legate of <a href="/wiki/Legio_III_Gallica" title="Legio III Gallica">Legio III Gallica</a>, had supported <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a> in his bid to the Imperial office and been rewarded with a suffect consulship, plus an ordinary one under <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a> in 85. The Aurelii Fulvii were therefore a relatively new senatorial family from <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Narbonensis" title="Gallia Narbonensis">Gallia Narbonensis</a> whose rise to prominence was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">Flavians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The link between Antoninus\' family and their home province explains the increasing importance of the post of <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">Proconsul</a> of Gallia Narbonensis during the late Second Century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus was born near <a href="/wiki/Lanuvium" title="Lanuvium">Lanuvium</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and his mother was Arria Fadilla. Antoninus&#8217; father died shortly after his 89 ordinary consulship, and Antoninus was raised by his maternal grandfather <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Arrius_Antoninus" title="Gnaeus Arrius Antoninus">Gnaeus Arrius Antoninus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._150_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._150-4">[4]</a></sup> reputed by contemporaries to be a man of integrity and culture and a friend of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger" title="Pliny the Younger">Pliny the Younger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-11">[11]</a></sup> The Arrii Antoninii were an older senatorial family from Italy, very influential during <a href="/wiki/Nerva" title="Nerva">Nerva</a>\'s reign. Arria Fadilla, Antoninus\' mother, married afterwards <a href="/wiki/Publius_Julius_Lupus" title="Publius Julius Lupus">Publius Julius Lupus</a>, suffect consul in 98; from that marriage came two daughters, Arria Lupula and Julia Fadilla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_children">Marriage and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Marriage and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Some time between 110 and 115, Antoninus married Annia Galeria <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Elder" title="Faustina the Elder">Faustina the Elder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius-13">[13]</a></sup> They are believed to have enjoyed a happy marriage. Faustina was the daughter of consul <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus" title="Marcus Annius Verus">Marcus Annius Verus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._150_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._150-4">[4]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rupilia" title="Rupilia">Rupilia</a> Faustina (a half-sister to the Empress <a href="/wiki/Vibia_Sabina" title="Vibia Sabina">Vibia Sabina</a>). Faustina was a beautiful woman, and despite (basically unproven) rumours about her character, it is clear that Antoninus cared for her deeply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Faustina bore Antoninus four children, two sons and two daughters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> They were:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Marcus Aurelius Fulvus Antoninus (died before 138); his sepulchral inscription has been found at the Mausoleum of Hadrian in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magie,_David_1921_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magie,_David_1921-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Marcus Galerius Aurelius Antoninus (died before 138); his sepulchral inscription has been found at the Mausoleum of Hadrian in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magie,_David_1921_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Magie,_David_1921-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> His name appears on a Greek Imperial coin.</li>\n<li>Aurelia Fadilla (died in 135); she married <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Plautius_Lamia_Silvanus" title="Lucius Plautius Lamia Silvanus">Lucius Plautius Lamia Silvanus</a>, consul 145. She appeared to have no children with her husband; and her sepulchral inscription has been found in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Annia Galeria Faustina Minor or <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina the Younger</a> (between 125&#8211;130&#8211;175), a future Roman Empress, married her maternal cousin <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> in 146.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._523_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._523-7">[7]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>When Faustina died in 141, Antoninus was greatly distressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In honour of her memory, he asked the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> to deify her as a goddess, and authorised the construction of a temple to be built in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a> in her name, with priestesses serving in her temple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> He had various coins with her portrait struck in her honor. These coins were scripted &#8216;<i>DIVA FAUSTINA</i>&#8217; and were elaborately decorated. He further created a charity which he founded and called it <i>Puellae Faustinianae</i> or <i>Girls of Faustina</i>, which assisted destitute girls <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius-13">[13]</a></sup> of good family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Finally, Antoninus created a new <i>alimenta</i> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_supply_to_the_city_of_Rome" title="Grain supply to the city of Rome">Grain supply to the city of Rome</a>).\n</p><p>The emperor never remarried.  Instead, he lived with Galena Lysistrata, one of Faustina\'s freed women. <a href="/wiki/Concubinage" title="Concubinage">Concubinage</a> was a form of female companionship sometimes chosen by powerful men in Ancient Rome, especially widowers like <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>. Their union could not produce any legitimate offspring who could threaten any heirs, such as those of Antoninus. Also, as one could not have a wife and an official concubine (or two concubines) at the same time, Antoninus avoided being pressed into a marriage with a noblewoman from another family (later, Marcus Aurelius would also reject the advances of his former fianc&#233;e Ceionia Fabia, Lucius Verus\'s sister, on the grounds of protecting his children from a stepmother, and took a concubine instead).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Favor_with_Hadrian">Favor with Hadrian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Favor with Hadrian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Having filled the offices of <a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a> with more than usual success,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> he obtained the consulship in 120.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius-13">[13]</a></sup> He was next appointed by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> as one of the four <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsuls</a> to administer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italia_(Roman_province)" title="Italia (Roman province)">Italia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._149_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._149-27">[27]</a></sup> then greatly increased his reputation by his conduct as <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a> of <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Asia</a>, probably during 134&#8211;135.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._149_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._149-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He acquired much favor with Hadrian, who adopted him as his son and successor on 25 February 138,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> after the death of his first adopted son <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Aelius" title="Lucius Aelius">Lucius Aelius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> on the condition that Antoninus would in turn adopt Marcus Annius Verus, the son of his wife\'s brother, and Lucius, son of Lucius Aelius, who afterwards became the emperors <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emperor">Emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1414" data-file-width="2052" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png/220px-Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png/330px-Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png/440px-Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Empire_in_150_AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman Empire during the reign of Antoninus Pius.</div></div></div>\n<p>On his accession, Antoninus\' name and style became <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">Imperator</a> <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a> Titus Aelius Hadrianus Antoninus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_(honorific)" title="Augustus (honorific)">Augustus</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontifex_Maximus" title="Pontifex Maximus">Pontifex Maximus</a></i>. One of his first acts as Emperor was to persuade the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> to grant divine honours to Hadrian, which they had at first refused;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._151_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._151-30">[30]</a></sup> his efforts to persuade the Senate to grant these honours is the most likely reason given for his title of <i>Pius</i> (dutiful in affection; compare <i><a href="/wiki/Pietas" title="Pietas">pietas</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Two other reasons for this title are that he would support his aged father-in-law with his hand at Senate meetings, and that he had saved those men that Hadrian, during his period of ill-health, had condemned to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._523_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._523-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Immediately after Hadrian\'s death, Antoninus approached Marcus and requested that his marriage arrangements be amended: Marcus\' betrothal to Ceionia Fabia would be annulled, and he would be betrothed to Faustina, Antoninus\' daughter, instead. Faustina\'s betrothal to Ceionia\'s brother Lucius Commodus would also have to be annulled. Marcus consented to Antoninus\' proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus built temples, theaters, and mausoleums, promoted the arts and sciences, and bestowed honours and financial rewards upon the teachers of <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius-13">[13]</a></sup> Antoninus made few initial changes when he became emperor, leaving intact as far as possible the arrangements instituted by Hadrian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._151_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._151-30">[30]</a></sup> Epigraphical and prosopographical research has revealed that Antoninus\' imperial ruling team centered around a group of closely knit senatorial families, most of them members of the priestly congregation for the cult of Hadrian, the <i>sodales Hadrianales</i>. According to the German historian <a href="/wiki/Hans-Georg_Pflaum" title="Hans-Georg Pflaum">H.G. Pflaum</a>, prosopographical research of Antoninus\' ruling team allows us to grasp the deeply conservative character of the ruling senatorial <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_non-military_reign">A non-military reign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: A non-military reign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1395" data-file-width="1459" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG/220px-RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG/330px-RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG/440px-RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomaForoRomanoTempioAntoninoFaustina.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The temple of Antoninus and <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Elder" title="Faustina the Elder">Faustina</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a> (now the church of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo_in_Miranda" title="San Lorenzo in Miranda">San Lorenzo in Miranda</a>). The emperor and his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augusta_(honorific)" title="Augusta (honorific)">Augusta</a></i> were deified after their death by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>There are no records of any military related acts in his time in which he participated. One modern scholar has written "It is almost certain not only that at no time in his life did he ever see, let alone command, a Roman army, but that, throughout the twenty-three years of his reign, he never went within five hundred miles of a legion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>His reign was the most peaceful in the entire history of the <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">Principate</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._525_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._525-35">[35]</a></sup> notwithstanding the fact that there were several military disturbances throughout the Empire in his time. Such disturbances happened in <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a> &#8211; where a senator was named as governor of <a href="/wiki/Mauretania_Tingitana" title="Mauretania Tingitana">Mauretania Tingitana</a> in place of the usual equestrian procurator<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and cavalry reinforcements from Pannonia were brought in,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> towns such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sala_Colonia" title="Sala Colonia">Sala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tipasa" title="Tipasa">Tipasa</a> being fortified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Similar disturbances took place in <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judea</a>, and amongst the <a href="/wiki/Brigantes" title="Brigantes">Brigantes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a>, none of them being considered serious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._525_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._525-35">[35]</a></sup> It was however in Britain that Antoninus decided to follow a new, more aggressive path, with the appointment of a new governor in 139, <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Lollius_Urbicus" title="Quintus Lollius Urbicus">Quintus Lollius Urbicus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._151_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._151-30">[30]</a></sup> a native of <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a> and previously governor of <a href="/wiki/Germania_Inferior" title="Germania Inferior">Germania Inferior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under instructions from the emperor, Lollius undertook an invasion of southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, winning some significant victories, and constructing the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a>. The wall, however, was soon gradually decommissioned during the mid-150s and eventually abandoned late during the reign (early 160s), for reasons that are still not quite clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._155_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._155-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Antonine\'s Wall is mentioned in just one literary source, Antoninus\' biography in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">Historia Augusta</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a> makes a brief and confused mention of a war in Britain. In one inscription honoring Antoninus, erected by <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Augusta" title="Legio II Augusta">Legio II Augusta</a>, which participated in the building of the Wall, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bridgeness_Slab" title="The Bridgeness Slab">relief</a> showing four naked prisoners, one of them beheaded, seems to stand for some actual warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_(Vatican).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3844" data-file-width="2240" decoding="async" height="378" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_%28Vatican%29.JPG/220px-0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_%28Vatican%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_%28Vatican%29.JPG/330px-0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_%28Vatican%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_%28Vatican%29.JPG/440px-0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_%28Vatican%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0_Antoninus_Pius_-_Museo_Chiaramonti_(Vatican).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Antonius Pius in military garb and <a href="/wiki/Muscle_cuirass" title="Muscle cuirass">muscle cuirass</a>, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_Chiaramonti" title="Museo Chiaramonti">Museo Chiaramonti</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vatican_Museums" title="Vatican Museums">Vatican Museums</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Antonine\'s Wall was, in principle, much shorter and at first sight more defensible than Hadrian\'s Wall, the additional area that it enclosed within the Empire was barren, with the effect that supply lines to it were strained enough that the costs from maintaining the additional territory outweighed the benefits of doing so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> It has been therefore speculated that the invasion of Lowland Scotland and the building of the wall had to do mostly with internal politics, that is, offering Antoninus an opportunity to gain some modicum of necessary military prestige at the start of his reign. Actually, the campaign in Britannia was followed by an Imperial salutation &#8211; that is, by Antoninus formally taking for the second (and last) time the title of <a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">Imperator</a> &#8211; in 142.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The fact that around the same time coins were struck announcing a victory in Britain points to Antoninus\' need to publicize his achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The orator <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Cornelius_Fronto" title="Marcus Cornelius Fronto">Fronto</a> was later to say that, although Antoninus bestowed the direction of the British campaign to others, he should be regarded as the helmsman who directed the voyage, whose glory, therefore, belonged to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>That this quest for some military achievement responded to an actual need is proved by the fact that, although generally peaceful, Antoninus\' reign was not free from attempts at usurpation: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">Historia Augusta</a> mentions two, made by the senators Cornelius Priscianus (by the way, Lollius Urbicus\' successor as governor of Britain) and Atilius Rufius Titianus &#8211; both confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Fasti_Ostienses" title="Fasti Ostienses">Fasti Ostienses</a> as well as by the erasing of Priscianus\' name from an inscription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In both cases, Antoninus was not in formal charge of the ensuing repression: Priscianus committed suicide and Titianus was found guilty by the Senate, with Antoninus abstaining from sequestering their families\' properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were also some troubles in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dacia_Inferior" title="Dacia Inferior">Dacia Inferior</a> which required the granting of additional powers to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Procurator_(Roman)" title="Procurator (Roman)">procurator</a> governor and the dispatch of additional soldiers to the province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._155_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._155-41">[41]</a></sup> On the Northern <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> coast, the Greek city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olbia,_Ukraine" title="Olbia, Ukraine">Olbia</a> was held against the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Also during his reign the governor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Germany" title="Upper Germany">Upper Germany</a>, probably Caius Popillius Carus Pedo, built new fortifications in the <a href="/wiki/Agri_Decumates" title="Agri Decumates">Agri Decumates</a>, advancing the <a href="/wiki/Limes_Germanicus" title="Limes Germanicus">Limes Germanicus</a> fifteen miles forward in his province and neighboring <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In the East, Roman suzerainty over <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> was retained by the choice in AD 140 of <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_dynasty_of_Armenia" title="Arsacid dynasty of Armenia">Arsacid</a> scion <a href="/wiki/Sohaemus_of_Armenia" title="Sohaemus of Armenia">Sohaemus</a> as client king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nevertheless, Antoninus was virtually unique among emperors in that he dealt with these crises without leaving Italy once during his reign,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> but instead dealt with provincial matters of war and peace through their governors or through imperial letters to the cities such as Ephesus (of which some were publicly displayed). This style of government was highly praised by his contemporaries and by later generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus was the last Roman Emperor recognised by the Indian Kingdoms. Raoul McLaughlin quotes Aurelius Victor as saying "The Indians, the Bactrians and the Hyrcanians all sent ambassadors to Antoninus. They had all heard about the spirit of justice held by this great emperor, justice that was heightened by his handsome and grave countenance, and his slim and vigorous figure." Due to the outbreak of the Antonine epidemic and wars against northern Germanic tribes, the reign of Marcus Aurelius was forced to alter the focus of foreign policies, and matters relating to the Far East were increasingly abandoned in favour of those directly concerning the Empire\'s survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_administration">Economy and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Economy and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg/220px-Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg/330px-Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg/440px-Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aureus_d%27Antonin_le_Pieux.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">aureus</a> of Antoninus Pius, 145 AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Antoninus was regarded as a skilled administrator and as a builder. In spite of an extensive building directive &#8211; the free access of the people of Rome to drinking water was expanded with the construction of aqueducts, not only in Rome but throughout the Empire, as well as bridges and roads &#8211; the emperor still managed to leave behind a sizable public treasury of around two and a half million <a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">sesterces</a> (Rome would not witness another Emperor leaving his successor with a surplus for a long time). But this treasury was depleted almost immediately after Antoninus\'s reign due to the plague brought back by soldiers after the Parthian victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Emperor also famously suspended the collection of taxes from cities affected by natural disasters, such as when fires struck Rome and Narbona, and earthquakes affected <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_of_Asia" title="Province of Asia">Province of Asia</a>. He offered hefty financial grants for rebuilding and recovery of various Greek cities after two serious earthquakes: the first, <i>circa</i> 140, which affected mostly Rhodes and other islands; the second, in 152, which hit <a href="/wiki/Cyzicus" title="Cyzicus">Cyzicus</a> (where the huge and newly built Temple to Hadrian was destroyed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a>. Antoninus\' financial help earned him praise by Greek writers such as <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Aristides" title="Aelius Aristides">Aelius Aristides</a> and Pausanias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> These cities received from Antoninus the usual honorific accolades, such as when he commanded that all governors of Asia should enter the province, when taking office, by way of Ephesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Ephesus was specially favoured by Antoninus, who confirmed and upheld its first place in the list of imperial honor titles, as opposed to Smyrna and <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his dealings with Greek-speaking cities, Antoninus followed the policy adopted by Hadrian of ingratiating himself with local elites, especially with local intellectuals: philosophers, teachers of literature, rhetoricians and physicians were explicitly exempted from any duties involving private spending for civic purposes &#8211; a privilege granted by Hadrian that Antoninus confirmed by means of an edict preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Digest_(Roman_law)" title="Digest (Roman law)">Digest</a> (27.1.6.8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Antoninus also created a chair for the teaching of rhetoric in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus was known as an avid observer of rites of religion and of formal celebrations &#8211; both Roman and foreign. He is known for having increasingly formalized the official cult offered to the <a href="/wiki/Cybele" title="Cybele">Great Mother</a>, which from his reign onwards included a bull sacrifice, a <a href="/wiki/Taurobolium" title="Taurobolium">taurobolium</a>, formerly only a private ritual, now being also performed for the sake of the Emperor\'s welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Antoninus also offered patronage to the worship of <a href="/wiki/Mithraism" title="Mithraism">Mithras</a>, to whom he erected a temple in <a href="/wiki/Ostia_Antica" title="Ostia Antica">Ostia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In 148, he presided over the celebrations of the 900th anniversary of the founding of Rome.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_reforms">Legal reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Legal reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians,_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus,_140-144_AD,_British_Museum_(16965588461).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1672" data-file-width="2433" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians%2C_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus%2C_140-144_AD%2C_British_Museum_%2816965588461%29.jpg/220px-Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians%2C_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus%2C_140-144_AD%2C_British_Museum_%2816965588461%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians%2C_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus%2C_140-144_AD%2C_British_Museum_%2816965588461%29.jpg/330px-Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians%2C_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus%2C_140-144_AD%2C_British_Museum_%2816965588461%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians%2C_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus%2C_140-144_AD%2C_British_Museum_%2816965588461%29.jpg/440px-Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians%2C_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus%2C_140-144_AD%2C_British_Museum_%2816965588461%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copy_inscribed_in_marble_of_a_letter_from_Antoninus_Pius_to_the_Ephesians,_from_the_Bouleuterion_at_Ephesus,_140-144_AD,_British_Museum_(16965588461).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copy inscribed in marble of a letter from Antoninus Pius to the Ephesians, from the <a href="/wiki/Bouleuterion" title="Bouleuterion">Bouleuterion</a> at Ephesus, 140&#8211;144 AD, explaining how the emperor resolved a dispute between the Roman cities of <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Antoninus tried to portray himself as a magistrate of the <i>res publica</i>, no matter how extended and ill-defined his competencies were. He is credited with the splitting of the imperial treasury, the <a href="/wiki/Fiscus" title="Fiscus">Fiscus</a>. This splitting had to do with the division of imperial properties into two parts: firstly, the fiscus itself &#8211; or <i>patrimonium</i>, meaning the properties of the "Crown", the hereditary properties of each succeeding person that sat on the throne, transmitted to his successors in office,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> regardless of their membership in the imperial family;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> secondly, the <i>res privata</i>, the "private" properties tied to the personal maintenance of the Emperor and his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> An anecdote in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">Historia Augusta</a> biography, where Antoninus replies to Faustina &#8211; who complained about his stinginess &#8211; that "we have gained an empire [and] lost even what we had before" possibly relates to Antoninus\' actual concerns at the creation of the <i>res privata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> While still a private citizen, Antoninus had increased his personal fortune greatly by mean of various legacies, the consequence &#8211; we are told &#8211; of his caring scrupulously for his relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>res privata</i> lands could be sold and/or given away, while the <i>patrimonium</i> properties were regarded as public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> It was a way of pretending that the Imperial function &#8211; and most properties attached to it &#8211; was a public one, formally subject to the authority of the Senate and the Roman people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> That the distinction played no part in subsequent political history &#8211; that the <i>personal</i> power of the princeps absorbed his role as office-holder &#8211; proves that the autocratic logic of the imperial order had already subsumed the old republican institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of the public transactions of this period there is only the scantiest of information, but, to judge by what is extant, those twenty-two years were not remarkably eventful in comparison to those before and after the reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-11">[11]</a></sup> However, Antoninus did take a great interest in the revision and practice of the law throughout the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._526_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._526-73">[73]</a></sup> One of his chief concerns was to having local communities conform their legal procedures to existing Roman norms: in a case concerning repression of banditry by local police officers ("irenarchs") in Asia Minor, Antoninus ordered that these officers should not treat suspects as already condemned, and also keep a detailed copy of their interrogations, to be used in the possibility of an appeal to the Roman governor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Also, although Antoninus was not an innovator, he would not always follow the absolute letter of the law; rather he was driven by concerns over humanity and equality, and introduced into <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> many important new principles based upon this notion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._526_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._526-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In this, the emperor was assisted by five chief lawyers: L. Fulvius Aburnius Valens, an author of legal treatises;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> L. Ulpius Marcellus, a prolific writer; and three others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._526_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._526-73">[73]</a></sup> These last three included L. Volusius Maecianus, a former military officer turned by Antoninus into a civil procurator, and who, in view of his subsequent career (discovered on the basis of epigraphical and prosopographical research), was the Emperor\'s most important legal adviser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Maecianus would eventually be chosen to occupy various prefectures (see below) as well as to conduct the legal studies of Marcus Aurelius. He was also the author of a large work on Fidei Commissa (Testamentary Trusts). As a hallmark of the increased connection between jurists and the imperial government,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Antoninus\' reign also saw the appearance of the <i>Institutes of Gaius</i>, an elementary legal manual for beginners (see <a href="/wiki/Gaius_(jurist)" title="Gaius (jurist)">Gaius (jurist)</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._526_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._526-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus passed measures to facilitate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfranchisement" title="Enfranchisement">enfranchisement</a> of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._527_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._527-78">[78]</a></sup> Mostly, he favoured the principle of <i>favor libertatis</i>, giving the putative freedman the benefit of the doubt when the claim to freedom was not clearcut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Also, he punished the killing of a slave by his/her master without previous trial<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and determined that slaves could be forcibly sold to another master by a <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a> in cases of consistent mistreatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Antoninus upheld the enforcement of contracts for selling of female slaves forbidding their further employment in prostitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In criminal law, Antoninus introduced the important principle that accused persons are <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">not to be treated as guilty before trial</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._527_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._527-78">[78]</a></sup> &#8211; as in the case of the irenarchs (see above). He also asserted the principle that the trial was to be held, and the punishment inflicted, in the place where the crime had been committed. He mitigated the use of <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> in examining slaves by certain limitations. Thus he prohibited the application of torture to children under fourteen years, though this rule had exceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._527_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._527-78">[78]</a></sup> However, it must be stressed that Antoninus <i>extended</i>, by means of a <a href="/wiki/Rescript" title="Rescript">rescript</a>, the use of torture as a means of obtaining evidence to pecuniary cases, when it had been applied up until then only in criminal cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Also, already at the time torture of free men of low status (<i>humiliores</i>) had become legal, as proved by the fact that Antoninus exempted town councillors expressly from it, and also free men of high rank (<i>honestiores</i>) in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One highlight during his reign occurred in 148, with the nine-hundredth anniversary of the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> being celebrated by the hosting of magnificent games in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> It lasted a number of days, and a host of exotic animals were killed, including elephants, giraffes, tigers, rhinoceroses, crocodiles and hippopotami. While this increased Antoninus\'s popularity, the frugal emperor had to debase the <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman currency</a>. He decreased the silver purity of the denarius from 89% to 83.5% &#8211; the actual silver weight dropping from 2.88&#160;grams to 2.68&#160;grams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman,_p._155_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman,_p._155-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scholars name Antoninus Pius as the leading candidate for an individual identified as a friend of Rabbi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judah_haNasi" title="Judah haNasi">Judah the Prince</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> (Avodah Zarah 10a&#8211;b), Rabbi Judah was very wealthy and greatly revered in Rome. He had a close friendship with "Antoninus", possibly Antoninus Pius,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> who would consult Rabbi Judah on various worldly and spiritual matters.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore,_Eleusis_(15552358684).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore%2C_Eleusis_%2815552358684%29.jpg/220px-The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore%2C_Eleusis_%2815552358684%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore%2C_Eleusis_%2815552358684%29.jpg/330px-The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore%2C_Eleusis_%2815552358684%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore%2C_Eleusis_%2815552358684%29.jpg/440px-The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore%2C_Eleusis_%2815552358684%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_ruins_of_the_East_Triumphal_Arch_built_by_Antoninus_Pius_outside_the_Sanctuary_of_Demeter_and_Kore,_Eleusis_(15552358684).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of the <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_arch" title="Triumphal arch">triumphal arch</a> of Antoninus Pius outside the Sanctuary of Demeter and Kore in <a href="/wiki/Eleusis" title="Eleusis">Eleusis</a>, Greece, imitating <a href="/wiki/Arch_of_Hadrian_(Athens)" title="Arch of Hadrian (Athens)">Hadrian\'s Arch in Athens</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 156, Antoninus Pius turned 70. He found it difficult to keep himself upright without <a href="/wiki/Corset" title="Corset">stays</a>. He started nibbling on dry bread to give him the strength to stay awake through his morning receptions. Marcus Aurelius had already been created consul with Antoninus in 140, receiving the title of <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a> &#8211; i.e., heir apparent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> As Antoninus aged, Marcus would take on more administrative duties, more still after the death&#8212;in 156 or 157&#8212;of one of Antoninus\' most trusted advisers, Gavius Maximus, who had been <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">praetorian prefect</a> (an office that was as much secretarial as military) for twenty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Gavius Maximus, who had been one of the most important members of Antoninus\' "team" of long standing advisers, had been awarded with the consular insignia and the honors due a senator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> He had developed a reputation as a most strict disciplinarian (<i>vir severissimus</i>, according to <i>Historia Augusta</i>) as well as some lasting grudges among fellow equestrian procurators &#8211; one of them, by predeceasing Gavius and vilifying him in his will, created a serious embarrassment to one of the heirs, the orator Fronto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Gavius Maximus\' death offered the opportunity to a welcome change in the ruling team, and it has been speculated that it was the legal adviser Volusius Maecianus who&#8212;after a brief spell as Praefect of Egypt, and a subsequent term as <a href="/wiki/Praefectus_annonae" title="Praefectus annonae">Praefectus annonae</a> in Rome &#8211; assumed the role of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89minence_grise" title="&#201;minence grise">grey eminence</a> precisely in order to prepare the incoming &#8211; and altogether new &#8211; joint succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In 160, Marcus and Lucius were designated joint consuls for the following year. Perhaps Antoninus was already ill; in any case, he died before the year was out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius_(Museo_del_Prado)_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1521" data-file-width="1210" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Antoninus_Pius_%28Museo_del_Prado%29_01.jpg/220px-Antoninus_Pius_%28Museo_del_Prado%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Antoninus_Pius_%28Museo_del_Prado%29_01.jpg/330px-Antoninus_Pius_%28Museo_del_Prado%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Antoninus_Pius_%28Museo_del_Prado%29_01.jpg/440px-Antoninus_Pius_%28Museo_del_Prado%29_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius_(Museo_del_Prado)_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The bust of Antoninus Pius at the <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Prado" title="Museo del Prado">Museo del Prado</a>, Madrid</div></div></div>\n<p>Two days before his death, the biographer reports, Antoninus was at his ancestral estate at <a href="/wiki/Lorium" title="Lorium">Lorium</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Etruria" title="Etruria">Etruria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> about twelve miles (19&#160;km) from Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Victor,_15:7_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Victor,_15:7-95">[95]</a></sup> He ate Alpine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gruyere" title="Gruyere">Gruyere</a> cheese at dinner quite greedily. In the night he vomited; he had a fever the next day. The day after that, 7 March 161,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> he summoned the imperial council, and passed the state and his daughter to Marcus. The emperor gave the keynote to his life in the last word that he uttered: when the <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribune</a> of the night-watch came to ask the password, he responded, "aequanimitas" (equanimity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._532_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._532-97">[97]</a></sup> He then turned over, as if going to sleep, and died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> His death closed out the longest reign since Augustus (surpassing <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> by a couple of months).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> His record for the second-longest reign would be unbeaten for 168 years, until 329 when it was surpassed by <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a>.\n</p><p>Antoninus Pius\' funeral ceremonies were, in the words of the biographer, "elaborate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> If his funeral followed the pattern of past funerals, his body would have been incinerated on a pyre at the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>, while his spirit would rise to the gods\' home in the heavens. However, it seems that this was not the case: according to his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">Historia Augusta</a> biography (which seems to reproduce an earlier, detailed report) Antoninus\' body (and not his ashes) was buried in <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant\'Angelo">Hadrian\'s mausoleum</a>. After a seven-day interval (<i>justitium</i>), Marcus and Lucius nominated their father for deification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In contrast to their behavior during Antoninus\' campaign to deify Hadrian, the senate did not oppose the emperors\' wishes. A <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">flamen</a></i>, or cultic priest, was appointed to minister the cult of the deified Antoninus, now <i>Divus Antoninus</i>.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Antoninus_Pius" title="Column of Antoninus Pius">column</a> was dedicated to Antoninus on the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-Weigel,_Antoninus_Pius-13">[13]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Antoninus_and_Faustina" title="Temple of Antoninus and Faustina">temple</a> he had built in the Forum in 141 to his deified wife Faustina was rededicated to the deified Faustina and the deified Antoninus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury,_p._532_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bury,_p._532-97">[97]</a></sup> It survives as the church of San Lorenzo in Miranda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_118_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_118-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomatic_mission_to_China">Diplomatic mission to China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Diplomatic mission to China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Sino-Roman relations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb,_Guixian,_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg/220px-Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg/330px-Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg/440px-Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Guixian%2C_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Green_glass_Roman_cup_unearthed_at_Eastern_Han_tomb,_Guixian,_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Green <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glass</a> cup unearthed from an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_Dynasty" title="Eastern Han Dynasty">Eastern Han Dynasty</a> (25&#8211;220 AD) tomb, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first group of people claiming to be an ambassadorial mission of Romans to China was recorded in 166 AD by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hou_Hanshu" title="Hou Hanshu">Hou Hanshu</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-103">[103]</a></sup> The embassy came to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huan_of_Han" title="Emperor Huan of Han">Emperor Huan</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_China" title="Han China">Han China</a> from "Andun" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">&#23433;&#25958;</span>; Emperor Antoninus Pius), "king of <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a>" (Rome). As Antoninus Pius died in 161, leaving the empire to his adoptive son <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius (Antoninus)</a>, and the envoy arrived in 166, confusion remains about who sent the mission given that both Emperors were named \'Antoninus\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The Roman mission came from the south (therefore probably by <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>), entering China by the frontier province of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rinan" title="Rinan">Rinan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tonkin" title="Tonkin">Tonkin</a> (present-day northern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>). It brought presents of <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a> <a href="/wiki/Horn_(anatomy)" title="Horn (anatomy)">horns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tortoise" title="Tortoise">tortoise</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_shell" title="Animal shell">shell</a>, probably acquired in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Asia" title="Southern Asia">Southern Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The text specifically states that it was the first time there had been direct contact between the two countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_2009,_p._27_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_2009,_p._27-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Furthermore, a piece of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glassware</a> has been found at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> tomb in <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> along the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>, dated to the early 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Roman golden medallions made during the reign of Antoninus Pius and perhaps even Marcus Aurelius have been found at <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="&#211;c Eo">&#211;c Eo</a> in southern Vietnam, then part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Kingdom of Funan</a> near the Chinese province of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_pp29-30_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_pp29-30-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborne_2006_pp24-25_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-osborne_2006_pp24-25-111">[111]</a></sup> This may have been the port city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kattigara" title="Kattigara">Kattigara</a>, described by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (c. 150) as being visited by a Greek sailor named Alexander and lying beyond the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Chersonese" title="Golden Chersonese">Golden Chersonese</a> (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_pp29-30_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_pp29-30-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborne_2006_pp24-25_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-osborne_2006_pp24-25-111">[111]</a></sup> Roman coins from the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> have been discovered in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>, China (site of the Han capital <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>), although the significantly greater amount of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Roman coins unearthed in India</a> suggest the Roman maritime trade for <a href="/wiki/History_of_silk" title="History of silk">purchasing Chinese silk</a> was centered there, not in China or even the overland <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> running through ancient Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sbeitla_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Sbeitla_04.jpg/220px-Sbeitla_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Sbeitla_04.jpg/330px-Sbeitla_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Sbeitla_04.jpg/440px-Sbeitla_04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sbeitla_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arch of Antoninus Pius in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sbe%C3%AFtla" title="Sbe&#239;tla">Sbe&#239;tla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_Palazzo_Altemps,_Rome_(11383990114).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="3216" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Antoninus_Pius%2C_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Rome_%2811383990114%29.jpg/220px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Rome_%2811383990114%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Antoninus_Pius%2C_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Rome_%2811383990114%29.jpg/330px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Rome_%2811383990114%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Antoninus_Pius%2C_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Rome_%2811383990114%29.jpg/440px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_Palazzo_Altemps%2C_Rome_%2811383990114%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_Palazzo_Altemps,_Rome_(11383990114).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Antoninus Pius, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palazzo_Altemps" title="Palazzo Altemps">Palazzo Altemps</a>, Rome</div></div></div>\n<p>The only intact account of his life handed down to us is that of the <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Augustan History</a></i>, an unreliable and mostly fabricated work. Nevertheless, it still contains information that is considered reasonably sound &#8211; for instance, it is the only source that mentions the erection of the Antonine Wall in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Antoninus is unique among Roman emperors in that he has no other biographies.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_later_scholarship">In later scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: In later scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Antoninus in many ways was the ideal of the landed gentleman praised not only by ancient Romans, but also by later scholars of classical history, such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> or the author of the article on Antoninus Pius in the <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica Eleventh Edition"><i>Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica</i> Eleventh Edition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A few months afterwards, on Hadrian\'s death, he was enthusiastically welcomed to the throne by the Roman people, who, for once, were not disappointed in their anticipation of a happy reign. For Antoninus came to his new office with simple tastes, kindly disposition, extensive experience, a well-trained intelligence and the sincerest desire for the welfare of his subjects. Instead of plundering to support his prodigality, he emptied his private treasury to assist distressed provinces and cities, and everywhere exercised rigid economy (hence the nickname &#954;&#965;&#956;&#953;&#957;&#959;&#960;&#961;&#953;&#963;&#964;&#951;&#962; "cummin-splitter"). Instead of exaggerating into treason whatever was susceptible of unfavorable interpretation, he turned the very conspiracies that were formed against him into opportunities for demonstrating his clemency. Instead of stirring up persecution against the Christians, he extended to them the strong hand of his protection throughout the empire. Rather than give occasion to that oppression which he regarded as inseparable from an emperor\'s progress through his dominions, he was content to spend all the years of his reign in Rome, or its neighbourhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p>\nSome historians have a less positive view of his reign. According to the historian <a href="/wiki/J._B._Bury" title="J. B. Bury">J. B. Bury</a>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>however estimable the man, Antoninus was hardly a great statesman. The rest which the Empire enjoyed under his auspices had been rendered possible through Hadrian&#8217;s activity, and was not due to his own exertions; on the other hand, he carried the policy of peace at any price too far, and so entailed calamities on the state after his death. He not only had no originality or power of initiative, but he had not even the insight or boldness to work further on the new lines marked out by Hadrian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>German historian <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Kornemann" title="Ernst Kornemann">Ernst Kornemann</a> has had it in his <i>R&#246;mische Geschichte</i> [2 vols., ed. by H. Bengtson, Stuttgart 1954] that the reign of Antoninus comprised "a succession of grossly wasted opportunities", given the upheavals that were to come. There is more to this argument, given that the Parthians in the East were themselves soon to make no small amount of mischief after Antoninus\' passing. Kornemann\'s brief is that Antoninus might have waged preventive wars to head off these outsiders. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Grant_(classicist)" title="Michael Grant (classicist)">Michael Grant</a> agrees that it is possible that had Antoninus acted decisively sooner (it appears that, on his death bed, he was preparing a large-scale action against the Parthians), the Parthians might have been unable to choose their own time, but current evidence is not conclusive. Grant opines that Antoninus and his officers did act in a resolute manner dealing with frontier disturbances of his time, although conditions for long-lasting peace were not created. On the whole, according to Grant, Marcus Aurelius\' eulogistic picture of Antoninus seems deserved, and Antoninus appears to have been a conservative and nationalistic (although he respected and followed Hadrian\'s example of Philhellenism moderately) Emperor who was not tainted by the blood of either citizen or foe, combined and maintained Numa Pompilius\' good fortune, pacific dutifulness and religious scrupulousness, and whose laws removed anomalies and softened harshnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Krzysztof Ulanowski argues that the claims of military inability are exaggerated, considering that although the sources praise Antoninus\' love for peace and his efforts "rather to defend, than enlarge the provinces", he could hardly be considered a pacifist, as shown by the conquest of the Lowlands, the building of the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a> and the expansion of Germania Superior. Ulianowski also praises Antoninus for being successful in deterrence by diplomatic means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Descendants">Descendants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Antoninus_Pius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Descendants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Although only one of his four children survived to adulthood, Antoninus came to be ancestor to four generations of prominent Romans, including the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hans-Georg_Pflaum" title="Hans-Georg Pflaum">Hans-Georg Pflaum</a> has identified five direct descendants of Antoninus and Faustina who were consuls in the first half of the third century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li>Marcus Aurelius Fulvus Antoninus (died before 138), died young without issue</li>\n<li>Marcus Galerius Aurelius Antoninus (died before 138), died young without issue</li>\n<li>Aurelia Fadilla (died in 135), who married <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Plautius_Lamia_Silvanus" title="Lucius Plautius Lamia Silvanus">Lucius Plautius Lamia Silvanus</a>, suffect consul in 145;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> no children known for certain.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina the Younger</a> (16 February between 125 and 130 &#8211; 175), had several children; those who had children were:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Annia Aurelia Galeria Lucilla</a> (7 March 150&#160;&#8211; 182?), whose children included:\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Pompeianus" title="Pompeianus">Tiberius Claudius Pompeianus</a></li></ol></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Annia_Galeria_Aurelia_Faustina" title="Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina">Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina</a> (151&#8211;?), whose children included:\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Claudius_Severus_Proculus" title="Tiberius Claudius Severus Proculus">Tiberius Claudius Severus Proculus</a>\n<ol><li>Empress <a href="/wiki/Annia_Faustina" title="Annia Faustina">Annia Faustina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus" title="Elagabalus">Elagabalus</a>\' third wife</li></ol></li></ol></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Fadilla" title="Fadilla">Fadilla</a> (159&#8211;?)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Annia_Cornificia_Faustina_Minor" title="Annia Cornificia Faustina Minor">Annia Cornificia Faustina Minor</a> (160&#8211;213)</li></ol></li></ol>\n<h2><span id="Nerva.E2.80.93Antonine_family_tree"></span><span class="mw-headline" class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin: 0.3em auto auto; clear:none; min-width:100%;padding-left:0.3em; width:100%;padding-left:0.3em; font-size:88%; border:1px solid #aaa;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th
